10.1016/j.jmp.2018.06.002
There are a variety of applications in which there is an interest in ranking a set of items using paired comparison data  (David, 1988) . Historically, psychological models of choice have played a key role, laying the foundations of the field and describing the structure in-which preference among objects are reflected by the choice probabilities of subjects  (Fishburn, 1973; Tversky & Russo, 1969; Yellott, 1977) . Today there is a large body of literature on paired comparisons in fields as diverse as psychology  (Davis-Stober, Park, Brown, & Regenwetter, 2016; Regenwetter, Dana, & Davis-Stober, 2011) , social choice theory  (Saari, 1995) , statistics  (Marden, 1996) , economics  (Blavatskyy, 2008; Debreu, 1958)  and computer science  (Furnkranz & Hullermeier, 2011) . Concrete examples include the ranking of chess players and other competitive athletes  (Govan, 2008; Herbrich, Minka, & Graepel, 2007) , the evaluation of political candidates  (Pacuit, 2012) , preference aggregation problems  (Ailon, Charikar, & Newman, 2008) , recommendation systems  (Cremonesi, Koren, & Turrin, 2010)  and many more. It is worth mentioning that models for paired comparisons are also related to the class of  Fechnerian  Models see  Dzhafarov and Colonius (1999, 2011) .

Axiomatic approaches have played an important role in the literature on paired comparisons and ranking. For example, axiomatic studies of preferences over random lotteries, which provide the foundations of expected utility theory, have received considerable attention in economics, e.g.,  Blavatskyy (2008) ,  Dagsvik (2005) ,  Neumann and Morgenstern (1944)  and  Ryan (2017) . Rank-aggregation problems, which arise in artificial intelligence and social choice theory  (Altman & Tennenholtz, 2008; Diaz, Hendrickx, & Lohmann, 2014)  have also been studied from an axiomatic perspective where the focus has been on providing impossibility theorems, in the spirit of Arrow’s Theorem, as well as the classification of ranking methods in terms of the properties which they satisfy. Our study is more closely related to that of  Debreu (1954, 1958)  and  Köbberling (2006)  where minimal conditions for expressing a preference relation using a real-valued utility function are provided. In particular the results of  Latta (1979)  are considerably extended in a similar spirit as  Ryan (2016)  and  Tversky and Russo (1969) .

Traditionally these are referred to as stochastic transitivity axioms or relations  (Fishburn, 1973) , although strictly speaking transitivity axioms simply specify different statistical models  (Cavagnaro & Davis-Stober, 2014) . Although there are applications for which weak stochastic transitivity (WST) is appropriate there are many others for which it is not. In particular WST allows for Condorcet type paradoxes  (Heckel, Shah, Ramachandran, & Wainwright, 2016) . For example, consider the following choice probabilities  
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               12 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               13 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         0 
                         . 
                         6 
                       and  
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               23 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         1 
                      . It is easy to verify that these probabilities satisfy WST and are consistent with the ranking  
                         1 
                         ≻ 
                         2 
                         ≻ 
                         3 
                      . Note, however, that the average choice probability of the second, and not the first, ranked item is the highest. This phenomena arises because the global ranking does not agree with the ranking derived from comparing items to a fixed standard.

Clearly,  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2

                               A 
                            
                            
                               1

                       for every

This eliminates Condorcet type paradoxes and provides sufficient structure for varied statistical analyses  (Chatterjee & Mukherjee, 2017; Heckel et al., 2016; Rajkumar & Agarwal, 2014; Shah, Balakrishnan, Guntoboyina, & Wainwright, 2017) . There are though, scenarios where SST might not be strong enough. Consider for example a knockout tournament where the seeding of players is random. One would expect that if  
                         i 
                         ≻ 
                         j 
                       then player  
                         i

                         j

As noted by a referee, stochastic transitivity by composition proposed by  Latta (1979)  is closely related to the  concatenation principle  from measurement theory, the  monotonicity axiom  in  Suppes, Krantz, Luce, and Tversky (1989)  and  weak bicancellation  in  Falmagne (2002) . Also, as pointed out by a referee, the work of  Latta (1979)  is closely related to Theorem 1 in Chapter 17 of  Suppes et al. (1989) .

A function  
                         F 
                       which satisfies  (1)  will be referred to as a comparison function. In the machine learning literature the comparison function is sometimes called the link function, whereas in the economics literature it is referred to as the contest success function  (Jia, Skaperdas, & Vaidya, 2013; Shalev-Shwartz & Ben-David, 2014) . We emphasize that the scores are in fact a realization of a mapping  
                         μ 
                         : 
                         J 
                         → 
                         R 
                       which satisfies  (1)  for every pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            i 
                            , 
                            j 
                            ) 
                         
                         ∈ 
                         J 
                      . When  
                         F 
                         ∈ 
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       the mapping  
                         μ 
                       can be viewed as a real-valued utility function  (Debreu, 1954, 1958) . There is a large collection of models that satisfy  (1) . These include the Bradley–Terry–Luce (BTL) models  (Ailon, 2008; Glickman & Hennessy, 2015; Shah, Balakrishnan, Bradley, Parekh, Ramchandran, & Wainwright, 2015; Zermelo, 1929) , Thurstone models  (Thurstone, 1927) , and others  (Batchelder, Bershal, & Simpson, 1992; Yellott, 1977) . One advantage of this class of models is that estimation of choice probabilities typically reduces to a convex optimization problem  (Tsukida & Gupta, 2011)  whereas estimation under  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       yield NP-Hard problems  (Chatterjee & Mukherjee, 2017; deCani, 1969; Shah et al., 2017)  as mentioned earlier. We also note that, under BTL, stronger players will always have better chances of winning knockout tournaments  (Chung & Hwang, 1978) , which, as noted earlier, cannot be guaranteed under  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       alone.

Axioms  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       describe local relations among triplets of choice probabilities. As such they may be viewed as primitives. As noted earlier, Axiom  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       is equivalent to the linear model  (1)  which completely describes the global structure of the choice probabilities. That is, given a vector of scores  
                         μ 
                       and a comparison function  
                         F 
                       the choice probabilities and the relationships among them are completely determined. Model  (1) , in contrast with  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                      , provides a convenient and structured framework in which paired comparison experiments can be studied. Thus, it is desirable to re-express  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       using a model whose building blocks are a set of merits and some generalized comparison function. Other theoretical challenges in the field involve clarifying the connections between Axioms  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       to

The main contribution of this paper is to derive more convenient functional equivalents of the axiomatic primitives  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          to  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                         . Doing so, provides global models in the spirit of  (1)  for Axioms  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         , thus extending considerably  (Latta, 1979) . Later on, we disassemble Axiom  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          into components that resemble  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         . In particular, new and elegant constructions of ordinal utility functions are provided. These are similar to the construction in  Debreu (1958)  without making use of the quadruple condition. In fact, under the conditions of  Debreu (1958) , if the quadruple condition is substituted for  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         , then there will exist an infinite subset of items which satisfies  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                         . Somewhat surprisingly, an equivalent statement does not hold under  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         . We also provide identifiability and uniqueness results for the new classes of functions, considerably extending  (Rockwell & Yellott, 1979; Yellott, 1977) . Throughout, we demonstrate the convenience of the functional representations. We do so by deriving equivalence relations between models that were previously inaccessible in the standard axiomatic forms. Proofs and additional results are provided in the  Appendix .

Our main findings are provided in this section. In particular we reformulate the axiomatic primitives  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       in terms of more convenient global linear models similar to  (1) . The new descriptions of  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       provide natural connections, both axiomatic and analytic, between transitivity models that were previously inaccessible using the standard formulation.

This subsection starts by showing, somewhat surprisingly, that any set of choice probabilities over a countable set of items may be written as a linear model. Constraints on the choice probabilities  
                            
                               
                                  p 
                               
                               
                                  i 
                                  j

                            F 
                          in  (1)  belongs to. We start with models which impose no constraints and define  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          to be the set of all symmetric functions  
                            F 
                            :

                            →

                               , 
                               1 
                               ]

Theorem 0  guarantees that  (1)  is satisfied for any countable set of items. Note that in this general settings the merits support a functional representation of the choice probabilities and do not necessarily represent an order relation, i.e.,

Theorem 1  states that, for countable sets, WST and interchangeability are jointly equivalent to the linear model  (1)  with a weak form of monotonicity. We note that once the class  
                            
                               
                                  F

                            F 
                            ∈ 
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  1

                            I 
                         , such a certificate will always exists for choice probabilities satisfying  
                            
                               
                                  A

                            J 
                          the smaller the set of valid certificates. Notice that in contrast with  Theorem 0 , the merits appearing in  Theorem 1  represent a preference relation, i.e.,  
                            i

In the above example, both functions  
                            F 
                          and  
                            G 
                          belong to the class  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         , thus,  
                            F 
                          and  
                            G 
                         , together with the corresponding merits serve as certificates that guarantee that the choice probabilities satisfy WST. The non-uniqueness of the certificate is closely related to the non-uniqueness of utility representations of preferences, see  Debreu (1954, 1958)  and  Kreps (2012)  for further discussions. Issues of uniqueness and identifiability  (Lehmann & Casella, 1998)  will be discussed in more detail in Section  2.4 .

Theorem 5  shows that, under  
                            I 
                         , the axiomatic primitive

Debreu’s Theorem:  
                         A preference relation  
                         
                            ⪰ 
                          
                         admits a real-valued utility representation if and only if there exists a countable subset  
                         
                            
                               
                                  J

                            ⊂ 
                            J 
                          
                         such that for every  
                         
                            i 
                            , 
                            j 
                            ∈ 
                            J 
                          
                         with  
                         
                            i 
                            ≻ 
                            j 
                          
                         there exist  
                         
                            k 
                            ∈ 
                            
                               
                                  J 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          
                         such that  
                         
                            i 
                            ⪰ 
                            k 
                            ≻ 
                            j 
                         
                         .

Debreu’s condition directly translates to choice probabilities.

Remark 
                            The assumption that the image of

                                  μ

                                        F 
                                     
                                     
                                        3 
                                     
                                  
                                holds (over the appropriate merit sets).

In contrast with  Theorems 9 and 10  the author of  Debreu (1958)  constructs LST using two conditions: ( i ) the quadruple condition, and; ( ii ) a condition similar to richness. We conclude this section with a variation of  Theorem 9  that applies to  sparse  sets of items. A set is called sparse if the associated items are order isomorphic to a subset of the integers. This means that for every pair of items  
                            i

                            j

                            J

Here, it may be useful to think of items as players, and choice probabilities as the probability of winning a match. Replicability states that if a weak player  
                            j 
                          can win against an opponent  
                            k 
                          with probability  
                            p 
                            ≥ 
                            1 
                            ∕ 
                            2 
                          then there is an opponent  
                            l 
                          against which a stronger player  
                            i 
                          can win with the same probability. When  
                            R 
                          holds, we say that choice probabilities of strong items span or replicate the choice probabilities of the weak items.

Theorem 12  provides natural conditions under-which linear stochastic transitivity emerges. It also provides a natural interpretation of  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          to  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                         . For example, consider the game of chess, where the set of players is clearly sparse. It seems natural that a stronger player  
                            i 
                          has better chances than a weaker player  
                            j 
                          of winning against any opponent  
                            k 
                         , this implies that SST holds. Furthermore, if stronger players are capable of replicating the matches of weaker players then LST holds. This is a theoretical justification for chess being an LST game.

As noted by a referee  Theorem 12  is intimately connected to the construction of cardinal utility in  Köbberling (2006) . Although the conclusions of  Köbberling (2006)  are similar to ours, the axiomatic underpinnings are very different. We take choice probabilities as primitives, whereas  Köbberling (2006)  takes a difference relation  
                            ⪰ 
                          over pairs of items as primitives. Moreover, our construction of LST avoids the “ standard sequence condition ” used in  Köbberling (2006) ; instead we show in our proof that this condition is obtained by our choice of axioms.

The purpose of this section is to explore issues related to identifiability and falsifiability, concepts that are explained below. In  Yellott (1977) , a notion of equivalence is provided, and in Theorem 2 of  Yellott (1977)  it is proven that two functions

Theorem 13  shows that for any class of functions  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          of cardinality smaller or equal to  
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        ℵ 
                                     
                                     
                                        0

                                  G 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          in  
                            
                               
                                  F

                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . For example, let  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          to be the set of all continuous functions. The cardinality of this set is exactly  
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        ℵ 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         . Therefore by  Theorem 13  there exists a function  
                            
                               
                                  G 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          that can generate any set of choice probabilities generated by the set of all continuous functions. The proof of  Theorem 13  involves an implicit construction of a function  
                            
                               
                                  G 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          from any given class  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . In general this construction yields an unwieldy function which is not monotone, continuous or invertible. One consequence of  Theorem 13  is that one can never discern whether a given set of choice probabilities was generated by a class  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          of interest, or by some other comparison function  
                            
                               
                                  G 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . Thus, the analysis of paired comparison data must always be performed within a specified, plausible class, of comparison functions. This is, of course, true for any class of statistical and more broadly scientific models  (Frigg & Hartmann, 2017) . Further note that given enough data it may be possible to identify when the choice probabilities do not belong to  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . Thus, we can never confirm that a parametric class is correctly specified, but we may be able to falsify such a hypothesis. We point out that a weaker version of  Theorem 13  also holds if one restricts the analysis to  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          alone, for this see  Rockwell and Yellott (1979) .

Note that Eqs.  (4)  and  (5)  may be well defined even when  
                            G 
                          is not sensitive and the choice probabilities cannot be re-expressed as a function in  
                            
                               
                                  F 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                         . Thus, the transformation in  (4)  provides a method of recovering the (hidden) LST model when it exists. When the underlying model is not LST the method produces an approximate LST model for the underlying SST model. In the following, three examples are provided to illustrate the application of  Theorem 14 . In the first we recover Theorem 2 of  Yellott (1977) , in the second we recover a well known LST model from a given  
                            G

                            G

This paper investigates the foundations of stochastic transitivity relations. We adopt an axiomatic approach to study the mathematical structures that emerge from modeling choice probabilities, in psychology, economics, statistics and others fields. This is done by first finding characterizations of the axiomatic primitives in terms of more convenient functional equivalents,  
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       that: (a) Provide a common mathematical structure for transitivity relations; (b) Reveal new relations and connections between transitivity models; (c) Enable various generalizations of known results; and (d) Furnish a foundation for new classes of statistical models.

In particular the functional representation allows for a set of items to be identified and compared by using a set of  
                         n 
                       merits rather than a  
                         n 
                         × 
                         n 
                       set of choice probabilities without any loss of descriptive power. A series of equivalence results between axiomatic primitives and corresponding functional classes are provided and relations with utility representations are explored. Our results provide an intimate connection between psychological models of choice and Debreu’s utility theory. The use of functional classes allows for various generalizations of known results, for example, we show how to obtain  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       from primitives without evoking the quadruple condition as constructed by  Debreu (1958) . The functional representation also allows the statement and proof of  Theorem 13  which shows that a model for paired comparison data can never be fully identified from the data.

Finally, in the appendix we also present some quantitative comparisons of transitivity models, typically associated with estimation and learning bounds, see  Appendix B . In addition we also show an example of how aggregation can play a role in actively enforcing stronger forms of transitivity. The relationship among modes of aggregation and modes of transitivity is an issue that deserves further investigation.

Many open problems remain. For example, to the best of our knowledge, the minimum level of transitivity required for stronger players to have better chances in fair knockout tournaments is still unknown. It is also unknown whether weaker forms of transitivity such as  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       can be re-expressed as a mixture of models in  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                      . The functional characterization of  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      , by the functional classes  
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       and in  
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      , suggests that new statistical models, both parametric and nonparametric, can be developed for these classes. This development has the potential of transforming NP-hard estimation  (Chatterjee & Mukherjee, 2017; deCani, 1969; Shah et al., 2017)  and inference problems into manageable convex problems. Throughout the paper we have assumed strong versions of  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                         . 
                       These assumptions come into play in various places in the paper. In particular, in  Theorems 10  to  12  the connection between  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       is made under additional assumptions. We believe that these connections could also be established even if the weak versions of  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       were used.

 Grants No.  1256/13  and  456/17 .

Aggregation  In many settings, the comparisons between pairs of items may be repeated multiple times or even chosen actively; we expect this to provide us additional information. Under the conditions of  Theorem 10  we will show that if the comparisons are repeated  
                         k 
                       times, for some  
                         k 
                         ∈ 
                         N 
                       fixed, then, the resulting comparison function that aggregates these comparisons will converge to a Gaussian cumulative function. Formally:

                                  0 
                                  ) 
                               
                             
                            alone.

Theorem 20  provides not only a natural construction of LST, but a specific comparison function known as the Thurstone model  (Thurstone, 1927) . The normal cumulative function obtained in  Theorem 20  is in fact ubiquitously used in the paired comparison literature ranging over various fields of psychometrics  (Morrison, 1963; Thurstone, 1927) , statistics  (Marden, 1996; Yellott, 1977) , economics  (Jia et al., 2013)  and others  (Langville & Meyer, 2012; Tsukida & Gupta, 2011) . Another possible aggregation scheme is to declare the item with the longest undefeated sequence of paired comparisons as the winner, in fact, many other aggregation schemes could be considered.
